15:574:52495:15:15:88:539:301:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:While the cost  shocksξjmare ultimately  a model  residual,  we present  evidence  in the supplemental
15:575:52597:15:16:72:539:286:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:appendix that the affiliations of co-owned newspapers are correlated, suggesting that these residuals may be
15:576:52705:16:15:72:540:270:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:thought of as partly capturing the personal political preferences of owners.  We model these cost shocks as
15:577:52812:15:15:72:539:255:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:proportional to the number of households.  Structurally, this reflects the idea that owners may value greater
15:578:52921:15:16:72:540:240:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:reach for their preferred ideologies. Practically, this assumption makes the affiliation choice game neutral to
15:579:53032:16:15:72:129:224:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:market scale.
15:580:53045:15:15:88:539:209:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:Each advertiser  earns  a revenue  equal  to the integral  overiof1nim≥1[ah+(nim−1)al],wherenimis

15:585:53567:15:16:72:539:133:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:in advertising impressions.  The model allows for the case of zero return to duplicate impressions (al=0)
15:586:53672:16:15:72:309:117:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:as well as the case of no diminishing returns (ah=al).

17:690:59029:15:16:72:540:225:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f1:B:firmsJ∗is the unique number such that entering newspapers are profitable but a marginal entrant would not

17:692:59146:27:0:71:285:182:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f7:B:(9)Vm(J∗)≥

17:697:59262:16:31:72:539:135:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:is the equilibrium.   Though we do not have a formal proof  thatVmmust be decreasing,  this condition  is
17:698:59367:31:11:72:539:104:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:advertising, thus raising the newspaper’s profits. Second, observe that in any equilibriumeach newspaper will charge a price such
17:699:59496:11:11:72:539:93:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:that advertisers are indifferent between advertising in that newspaper and not; otherwise the newspaper could raise its advertising

17:701:59759:11:0:71:313:71:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:price is given by (6) for all newspapers, implying the desired result.
18:702:59831:0:16:72:539:709:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:intuitive: it means that all else equal afirm would rather be in a market with fewer competitors. In repeated
18:703:59940:16:31:72:500:693:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:simulations wefind that this property holds for all markets in our data at the estimated parameters.
18:704:60040:31:23:72:153:662:143:Times:143:Times:C:5.4  Discussion
18:705:60055:23:23:72:155:639:119:Times:119:Times:C:Market Definition
18:706:60072:23:15:72:539:616:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:We make  two important  simplifying  assumptions  in defining  newspaper  markets.   First,  we assume  that
18:707:60180:15:15:72:540:601:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:newspapers only compete with other newspapers headquartered in the same market, and we ignore circula-
18:708:60282:15:16:72:540:586:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:tion in hinterland towns in modeling newspapers’ affiliation, pricing and entry choices.  In reality, the 1924
18:709:60392:16:15:72:540:570:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:ABC data shows that  home-market  papers  constitute  90 percent  of circulation  in news markets,  and the
18:710:60499:15:15:72:539:555:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:average newspaper sold 65 percent of copies in its home market.12Our definition is thus an approximation
18:711:60603:15:16:72:540:540:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:to a reality in which consumers exhibit strong but not exclusive preferences for local papers.  To assess ro-
18:712:60712:16:15:72:540:524:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:bustness to this assumption, we show results in appendix A from a subsample that excludes markets close to
18:713:60818:15:15:72:539:509:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:large cities, and from a specification in which we incorporate hinterland towns into our measure of market
18:714:60924:15:16:72:112:494:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:ideology.
18:715:60933:16:15:88:539:478:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:Second, we aggregate all substitutes for daily newspapers into an outside option whose prices and char-
18:716:61036:15:15:72:540:463:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:acteristics we do not model explicitly.  We deliberately choose a period of study in which there were few
18:717:61141:15:16:72:540:448:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:such substitutes that were also significant source of political information.  In 1924, television did not exist
18:718:61252:16:15:72:540:432:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:and radio was in its infancy as a news source (Sterling and Kittross 2001). Although weekly newspapers and
18:719:61358:15:15:72:540:417:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:magazines existed and played an important role in the media market, neither conveyed the news on a daily
18:720:61462:15:15:72:540:402:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:basis, and neither weekly newspapers nor weekly magazines achieved total weekly circulation in excess of
18:721:61566:15:32:72:330:387:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:the totaldailycirculation of daily newspapers (Field 2006).
18:722:61625:32:22:72:183:355:119:Times:119:Times:C:Product Characteristics
18:723:61648:22:16:72:540:333:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:Our  model  endogenizes  political  affiliation  but  not  other  forms  of differentiation.   This  is clearly  a dra-
18:724:61767:16:15:72:539:317:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:matic simplification, as variation in both quality and non-political horizontal dimensions (such as time of
18:725:61874:15:15:72:540:302:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:publication)  was clearly important.  Estimating consumer preferences  for other dimensions is straightfor-
18:726:61981:15:16:72:539:287:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:ward, but endogenizing newspapers’ choices of attributes along multiple dimensions would add significant
18:727:62085:16:15:72:123:271:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:complexity.
18:728:62096:15:15:88:540:256:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:Failing to account  for unobserved  vertical differentiation  can lead to bias in price coefficients (Berry
18:729:62203:15:16:72:539:241:119:g_d0_f1:121:g_d0_f8:B:et  al.   1995).   As  we  detail  further  below,  this  concern  motivates  us  to  identify  the  price  coefficientα
18:730:62323:16:15:72:539:225:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:from  the  monopolyfirst-order  condition  (Gentzkow  2007)  rather  than  from  price  variation.   We present
18:731:62434:15:15:72:540:210:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:several  additional  sensitivity  analyses.   In  appendix  A  we show  results  from  a  model  that  allows  utility
18:732:62552:15:16:72:540:195:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:to depend on distance to a newspaper’s headquarters, an important shifter of quality.  We also experiment
18:733:62657:16:15:72:539:179:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:with  specifications  that  use  a newspaper’s  price  and  home  market  circulation  as quality  proxies.   In  the
18:734:62774:15:15:72:540:164:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:supplemental appendix, we show explicitly that the crucial cross-sectional patterns that identify our demand
18:735:62882:15:41:72:438:149:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:system are robust to allowingflexibly for variation in quality at the newspaper level.
18:736:62968:41:15:84:540:108:49:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:12Fan (2013) uses a wider market definition that encapsulates 85 percent of a newspaper’s circulation and includes circulation
18:737:63094:15:11:71:539:93:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:outside a newspaper’s home market. Allowing for this additional realism would be difficult in our model as we estimate newspapers’
18:738:63224:11:11:71:539:82:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:incentive  to  enter  the  market.   Doing  so  in  a  model  with  overlapping  markets  would  mean  computing  post-entry  equilibrium
18:739:63361:11:0:71:506:71:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:configurations taking account of strategic linkages of distant newspapers through chains of partially overlapping markets.
19:740:63485:0:16:88:539:709:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:Ignoring  non-political  horizontal  differentiation  is also an important  simplification.   The more news-
19:741:63593:16:15:72:540:693:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:papers can differentiate on non-political dimensions, the weaker will be their incentive to differentiate on
19:742:63701:15:15:72:540:678:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:politics  (Irmen and Thisse 1998;  Liu and Shuai 2013).   We expect  our  empirical  estimates  to reflect the
19:743:63811:15:16:72:540:663:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:incentive  to differentiate  on political  dimensionsgiventhe extent  of differentiation  on other dimensions.
19:744:63921:16:15:72:540:647:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:As we do not treat differentiation  on other dimensions as endogenous,  we cannot allow it to vary in our
19:745:64026:15:15:72:540:632:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:counterfactuals,  which could impact our conclusions.   For example,  we could overstate or understate  the
19:746:64133:15:16:72:540:617:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:welfare effects of collusion, because collusion can both encourage and discourage non-political differentia-
19:747:64241:16:15:72:539:601:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:tion (Sweeting 2010). In addition, we could overstate the value of variety, because we capture non-political
19:748:64349:15:15:72:413:586:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:differentiation through a symmetric logit error (Ackerberg and Rysman 2005).
19:749:64425:15:16:88:539:571:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:In the supplemental appendix we present sensitivity analyses related to these concerns. We show that our
19:750:64529:16:15:72:539:555:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f1:B:finding that variety is undersupplied survives even if we exogenously cap the number of entering newspapers.
19:751:64637:15:15:72:540:540:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:We also  present  results  from  an experiment  in which we simulate  data  from  a model  with two types  of
19:752:64746:15:15:72:540:525:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:horizontal  differentiation–politics  and time of publication–and  estimate a misspecified  model that allows
19:753:64855:15:16:72:539:510:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:only for political differentiation.  Wefind that the estimated model matches the qualitative counterfactual
19:754:64962:16:15:72:540:494:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:predictions of the data-generating model well, although there are some quantitative differences between the
19:755:65069:15:31:72:91:479:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:two.
19:756:65073:31:23:72:177:448:119:Times:119:Times:C:Consumer Preferences
19:757:65093:23:15:72:539:425:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:Our demand specification is designed to capture two key elements: consumers’ preferences for like-minded
19:758:65197:15:16:72:540:410:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:political news, and the possibility of a given household reading multiple papers.  The former is obviously
19:759:65303:16:15:72:540:394:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:important given our focus on political differentiation. The latter is equally crucial, because audience duplica-
19:760:65415:15:15:72:540:379:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:tion across papers will be the key driver of advertising competition. It is also consistent with our readership
19:761:65526:15:16:72:355:364:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:surveys, which show a significant amount of multiple readership.
19:762:65590:16:15:88:540:348:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:Our demand model nests several cases of interest.  Whenβ=βandΓd=Γs=0, it is equivalent to a
19:763:65681:15:15:72:543:333:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:model in which each newspaper is a monopolist facing logit demand.  Whenβ=βandΓd,Γs→∞,itisa
19:764:65772:15:16:72:539:318:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:standard logit model in which each household reads at most one newspaper. Whenβ→−∞, it is equivalent
19:765:65872:16:15:72:508:302:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:to a model in which there are two distinct markets, one forRnewspapers and one forDnewspapers.
19:766:65966:15:15:88:539:287:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:An important simplifying assumption is that we allow only two types of consumers:  Republican and
19:767:66063:15:15:72:539:272:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:Democratic.  In reality, of course, some consumers in the period we study did not have a definite partisan
19:768:66169:15:16:72:539:257:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:affiliation, although this group was likely in a minority.13In the presence of unmodeled nonpartisan con-
19:769:66274:16:15:72:540:241:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:sumers, we expect that the gapβ−βthat we estimate will measure the “average” level of partisanship in
19:770:66375:15:15:71:539:226:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:news preferences, i.e. a value between the preferences of partisans and nonpartisans.  The supplemental ap-
19:771:66482:15:16:71:540:211:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:pendix presents estimates of an augmented demand system that allows for politically unaffiliated consumers,
19:772:66589:16:76:71:427:195:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:and shows how our counterfactual estimates change with the fraction unaffiliated.
19:773:66670:76:15:84:540:119:49:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:13Using Burnham’s (1965) index based on aggregate election returns, Rusk (1970) estimates that split-ticket voting in the US
19:774:66794:15:11:71:539:104:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:was not more than seven percent during the period 1876-1908.  Millspaugh (1918) reports based on actual ballot records that 21.7
19:775:66922:11:11:71:539:93:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:percent of votes in Rhode Island in 1906 were split-ticket.  Erikson and Tedin (1981) report that 26 percent of voters switched
19:776:67049:11:11:71:539:82:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:parties between the 1924 and 1928 elections and that thisfigure fell to 11 percent by 1944.  (The 1924-28 period coincides with a
19:777:67178:11:0:71:188:71:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:major shift in US party politics.)
20:778:67214:0:23:72:157:709:119:Times:119:Times:C:Advertising Game
20:779:67230:23:15:72:540:686:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:Our model of advertising competition draws heavily on the theoretical literature on competition in two-sided
20:780:67338:15:16:72:540:671:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:markets with multi-homing (Armstrong 2002; Ambrus and Reisinger 2006; Anderson et al.  2011).  Allow-
20:781:67439:16:15:72:540:655:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:ing for advertising competition is important because advertising accounted for the majority of newspaper
20:782:67543:15:15:72:227:640:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:revenue during the period we study.
20:783:67578:15:16:88:540:625:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:The  prediction  of  diminishing  returns  to  duplicate  impressionsfits  with  narrative  evidence  from  the
20:784:67689:16:15:72:540:609:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:period we study.  It was common for advertisers to assess the duplication in readership across publications
20:785:67796:15:15:72:540:594:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:when considering where to place ads, and to consider duplicate impressions to the same household to be less
20:786:67903:15:16:72:540:579:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:valuable than unique impressions.14Indeed, these practices explain the existence of the readership surveys
20:787:68009:16:15:72:538:563:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:that we use for a portion of our analysis, which were typically sponsored by one or more local newspapers.
20:788:68115:15:15:88:539:548:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:Our advertising  model  makes several  important  simplifying  assumptions.   First,  we do not allow the
20:789:68220:15:16:72:540:533:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:quantity of ads to affect the utility of a newspaper to consumers.  In contrast to the literature on broadcast
20:790:68330:16:15:72:540:517:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:media, for print media the evidence is mixed on consumers’ valuations of advertising, with good empirical
20:791:68435:15:15:72:540:502:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:support for a positive value to readers in some settings (Bogart 1981; Kaiser and Wright 2006; Sonnac 2000;
20:792:68542:15:16:72:540:487:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:Kaiser and Song 2009).  Implicitly, our approach follows Dertouzos and Trautman (1990) in assuming the
20:793:68644:16:15:72:540:471:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:consumer treats advertising and news content symmetrically. Second, we assume that advertisers’ valuations
20:794:68750:15:15:72:509:456:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:are homogeneous and do not depend on consumer types (as they do, for example, in Chandra 2009).
20:795:68845:15:15:88:539:441:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:We impose these restrictions because we do not have reliable cross-sectional data on advertising quan-
20:796:68947:15:16:71:539:426:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:tities and rates.  The most important consequence of these assumptions is that all advertisers are served in
20:797:69055:16:15:71:539:410:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:equilibrium in the model, which means that the advertising side of the market is allocatively efficient even
20:798:69163:15:15:71:539:395:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:under imperfect competition.  This is a strong assumption.  However, we note that because display adver-
20:799:69267:15:16:71:539:380:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:tising rates are often negotiated individually, newspapers have substantial scope for price discrimination in
20:800:69376:16:15:71:538:364:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:rates, which means that imperfect competition may not lead to quantity restrictions relative to thefirst-best.
20:801:69486:15:15:88:539:349:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:We also assume that newspaper costs are independent of the number of ads printed. This is primarily for
20:802:69589:15:16:71:539:334:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:simplicity. Equilibrium advertising prices and quantities would be unchanged if we allowed for a per-reader
20:803:69696:16:15:71:456:318:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:cost of printing each advertiser’s ad, provided the printing cost per reader is less thanal.
20:804:69788:15:15:88:540:303:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:For ease of exposition, we assume that circulation prices arefixed at the timefirms set advertising rates.
20:805:69894:15:16:71:539:288:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:This is consistent with the fact that subscription prices are typically posted (and so adjust relatively infre-
20:806:70005:16:15:71:539:272:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:quently), whereas advertising rates are often negotiated.  However, because advertising prices do not affect
20:807:70113:15:15:71:539:257:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:consumer demand, the equilibria of the game we study are equivalent to those of a game with simultaneous
20:808:70217:15:31:71:284:242:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:choice of both circulation and advertising prices.
20:809:70267:31:23:71:203:211:119:Times:119:Times:C:Entry and Affiliation Games
20:810:70294:23:15:71:539:188:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:Our model of entry and affiliation choice is shaped by three main considerations.  First, to credibly identify
20:811:70404:15:16:71:539:173:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:the strategic interactions offirms that share a market environment, it is important to allow for unobservable
20:812:70513:16:15:71:539:157:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:shocks  at  both  the  market  and  individualfirm level  (Brock  and  Durlauf  2007;  Aguirregabiria  and  Nevo
20:813:70625:15:15:71:539:142:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:2013).  Second, to take advantage of our data, we want the model to reflect the reality that entry in these
20:814:70732:15:30:71:539:127:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:markets happened sequentially, and that we know the order in which it occurred. Third, as a practical matter,
20:815:70841:30:15:84:539:97:49:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:14In his text on advertising campaigns, Martin (1921) writes that “The same advertisement seen in two or three newspapers is
20:816:70965:15:11:71:540:82:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:certainly more effective than if seen in one, but some advertisers are convinced that it is not worth three times as much to have an
20:817:71097:11:0:71:414:71:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:advertisement seen in three papers, reaching largely the same readers, as to have it seen in one.”
21:818:71197:0:16:72:444:709:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:we want to avoid complications related to estimating models with multiple equilibria.
21:819:71282:16:15:88:539:693:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:To meet these goals and still maintain tractability, we combine a relatively rich incomplete information
21:820:71386:15:15:72:539:678:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:model of affiliation choice with a more stylized complete-information model of the entry stage in the style
21:821:71493:15:16:72:539:663:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:of Bresnahan and Reiss (1991).   In the affiliation stage,firms’ decisions  are based on both idiosyncratic
21:822:71600:16:15:72:540:647:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:and market-level  unobservables.   They condition  on the choices  of past  movers,  and take account  of the
21:823:71709:15:15:72:539:632:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:way their decisions will affect those who come later.  In the entry stage, by contrast,firms do not yet know
21:824:71817:15:16:72:540:617:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:their idiosyncratic shocks, and so they are all symmetric. Equilibrium is determined by a simple optimality
21:825:71924:16:15:72:529:601:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:condition, though it still requires numerical integration over the market-level shock as in Mazzeo (2002).
21:826:72030:15:15:88:540:586:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:An important departure from Bresnahan and Reiss (1991) is that we allow the distribution offixed costs
21:827:72132:15:16:71:539:571:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:to depend on market size.  We do this because newspapers’fixed investments, notably editorial costs, are
21:828:72236:16:15:71:539:555:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:endogenous to the quality of the newspaper and hence to the size of the market served (Berry and Waldfogel
21:829:72342:15:15:71:540:540:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:2010).  In section 8 we report evidence that our estimates of thefixed costs of newspapers of different size
21:830:72450:15:15:71:539:525:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:are a good match to the data. In appendix A we show that ourfindings are robust to allowing a moreflexible
21:831:72556:15:16:72:329:510:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:dependence of the distribution offixed costs on population.
21:832:72615:16:15:88:539:494:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:Among the many restrictive assumptions our model embeds, two are particularly important.  First, we
21:833:72714:15:15:72:539:479:119:g_d0_f1:64:Times:B:assume  that  entry  decisions  precede  affiliation  decisions,  withfirms only  learning  their  cost  shocksξjm
21:834:72828:15:16:72:539:464:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:post-entry.15This is clearly an abstraction.  The most important substantive cost is that we shut down entry
21:835:72936:16:15:72:539:448:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:deterrence incentives in affiliation choice. The benefits are that we simplify computation dramatically, since
21:836:73046:15:15:72:540:433:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:we need only backward induct through the sequential game for the set of actual entrants rather than the full
21:837:73154:15:16:72:540:418:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:set of potential entrants,  and that our estimates are not sensitive to assumptions about the set of potential
21:838:73264:16:15:72:540:402:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:entrants.  The latter is particularly important in our case, since we have almost no evidence on the nature of
21:839:73374:15:15:72:265:387:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:this pool or the way it varies across markets.
21:840:73420:15:16:88:539:372:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:Second, we approximate a dynamic entry process by a static model. Although we capture some aspects
21:841:73518:16:15:72:539:356:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:of the dynamics by making affiliation choice sequential, we abstract from the reality that one entrant typ-
21:842:73625:15:15:72:540:341:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:ically operates in the market for a substantial  time before the next entrant arrives, thatfirms do exit, and
21:843:73734:15:16:72:539:326:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:that different entrants face different demand conditions when choosing affiliations. The most obvious chal-
21:844:73841:16:15:72:540:310:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:lenge in moving to a dynamic model is the computational difficulty of allowing for both market-level and
21:845:73945:15:37:72:325:295:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f1:B:firm-level unobservables (Aguirregabiria and Nevo 2013).

23:965:79304:16:15:72:539:562:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:the monopolyfirst-order condition to identify the price coefficient because we lack compelling exclusion
23:966:79408:15:15:72:510:547:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:restrictions but we have reasonably good information on variable markups for the typical newspaper.
23:967:79507:15:16:88:539:532:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:The relationship between the share of a town’s available newspapers that are Republican andZtidentifies

23:972:79953:15:15:72:540:455:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:ofσtownν. Given this parameter, the within-town relationship between the relative circulation of Republican

23:974:80172:15:16:72:540:425:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:is  shown  in  table  3.   The  more  increasing  the  number  of  Republican  newspapers  decreases  the  relative

24:1028:83247:15:15:88:539:369:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:Given these parameters, the relationship shown in table 4 between the numbers of Republican and Demo-
24:1029:83348:15:16:72:539:354:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:cratic incumbents and the choices of entrants identifies the advertising parameteral. This parameter captures
24:1030:83457:16:15:72:540:338:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:the extent of diminishing returns in advertising, and thus the extent to which newspapers earn less on over-

24:1032:83604:15:16:88:539:308:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:The demand estimates reported below imply that overlap in readership is greater between newspapers of
24:1033:83705:16:15:72:539:292:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:thesameaffiliation than newspapers of a different affiliation. Given this fact, lower values ofalcorrespond
24:1034:83812:15:15:72:539:277:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:to a stronger incentive to differentiate in order to soften advertising competition, soalis identified by the
24:1035:83921:15:16:72:540:262:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:extent  to  which  newspapers  differentiate  more  than  would  be  expected  from  the  demand  system  alone,
24:1036:84033:16:15:72:539:246:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:along with information from the entry patterns on the extent to which per-newspaper profits decline with the
24:1037:84141:15:15:72:173:231:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:number of newspapers.
24:1038:84162:15:16:88:465:216:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:The scale termσξis identified by residual variation in newspapers’ affiliation choices.
24:1039:84249:16:15:88:539:200:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:The parameters of thefixed cost distribution are then pinned down by correlation between the number
24:1040:84348:15:15:72:540:185:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:of newspapers and the market’s population,  which determinesμ0κandμ1κ,  and the extent  of variation in
24:1041:84451:15:15:72:538:170:119:g_d0_f1:64:g_d0_f8:B:the number of newspapers conditional on population, which determinesσκ.  The dispersion parameterσκ
24:1042:84550:15:16:72:540:155:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:determines how much the equilibrium number of newspapers responds to changes in profits induced by the
24:1043:84652:16:42:72:195:139:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:counterfactuals we consider.

25:1049:85140:37:27:72:152:656:206:Times:206:Times:C:8Results
25:1050:85148:27:23:72:363:629:143:Times:143:Times:C:8.1  Parameter Estimates and Determinants of Diversity
25:1051:85202:23:15:72:540:606:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:Tables  5  and  6  report  estimates  of  demand  and  supply  parameters,  respectively,  along  with  asymptotic
25:1052:85316:15:15:72:540:591:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:standard errors.  In the supplemental appendix, we present Monte Carlo experiments and experiments with
25:1053:85419:15:16:72:292:576:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:random starting values for both sets of parameters.
25:1054:85470:16:15:88:539:560:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:The qualitative patterns in both sets of parameters accord with economic intuition and the descriptive
25:1055:85572:15:15:72:539:545:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:evidence in tables 3 and 4. On the demand side, households prefer newspapers whose affiliations match their
25:1056:85679:15:16:72:540:530:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:own.  Bundles of newspapers produce less utility than the sum of the utilities produced by the component
25:1057:85783:16:15:72:539:514:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:papers alone,  and these diminishing  returns are greater  for same-type newspapers  than for opposite-type
25:1058:85890:15:15:72:540:499:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:newspapers.   There  is  substantial  unobserved  heterogeneity  in  household  ideology  across  towns,  which
25:1059:86001:15:16:72:540:484:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:in turn  is correlated  with  the fraction  of  available  newspapers  that  are Republican.   On the  supply  side,
25:1060:86117:16:15:72:539:468:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:advertising rates are lower for overlapping readers than for singleton readers, and unobserved heterogeneity
25:1061:86225:15:15:72:153:453:119:g_d0_f1:64:g_d0_f1:B:is less important.19
25:1062:86245:15:16:88:539:438:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:Our model implies that readership overlaps more between papers of the same affiliation than papers of
25:1063:86346:16:15:72:540:422:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:different affiliations, a fact consistent with evidence reported in the supplemental appendix from our reader-
25:1064:86456:15:15:72:540:407:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:ship surveys. For example, in data simulated from our model for two-paper markets, the average readership
25:1065:86561:15:15:72:539:392:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:overlap  for same-affiliation  papers  is 17 percent,  compared  to 14 percent  for opposite-affiliation  papers.
25:1066:86674:15:16:72:540:377:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:This results from the strong taste for like-minded news (β−β) outweighing the greater substitutability of
25:1067:86779:16:15:72:539:361:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:same-affiliation papers (Γs>Γd).20As noted above, it implies that advertising competition will increase
25:1068:86882:15:15:72:231:346:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:incentives to differentiate politically.
25:1069:86922:15:16:88:540:331:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:The estimated parameters of thefixed cost distribution appear reasonable. In simulation wefind that the
25:1070:87025:16:15:72:539:315:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:meanfixed cost of monopoly newspapers is $9.03 per copy, as against $7.73 in the Inland Press data.  The
25:1071:87129:15:15:72:540:300:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:concept measured by the model incorporates sunk costs and opportunity costs that may not be reflected in
25:1072:87233:15:16:72:539:285:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f1:B:financial data, so it is intuitive that the estimatedfixed costs are somewhat higher than those in the Inland
25:1073:87342:16:15:72:539:269:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:Press data.  The model implies thatfixed costs per capita decline very slowly with the size of the market: a
25:1074:87450:15:15:72:539:254:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:ten percent increase in population reducesfixed costs per capita by only 6 cents. This is consistent with the
25:1075:87559:15:15:72:539:239:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:Inland Press data, which show essentially no relationship betweenfixed costs per copy and the number of
25:1076:87662:15:16:72:123:224:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:copies sold.
25:1077:87674:16:15:88:540:208:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:In the supplemental appendix, we present estimates of the main regression specifications in tables 3 and
25:1078:87778:15:15:72:539:193:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:4 using data simulated from the model at the estimated parameters. We also present afigure illustrating the
25:1079:87885:15:16:72:540:178:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f1:B:fit of the entry model.  These regressions andfigure show that the estimated modelfits key features of the
25:1080:87991:16:15:72:540:162:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:data well on the whole. An important exception is that the model under-predicts the number of large markets
25:1081:88098:15:17:72:540:147:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:with two papers relative to the number with one or three, possibly due to the functional form imposed by
25:1082:88202:17:14:84:539:130:49:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:19The fact that unobservables are less important in the supply model than in the demand model may come from the fact that
25:1083:88323:14:11:71:539:116:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:county vote share is a better proxy for the ideology of large markets than of small towns.  In the supplemental appendix we show
25:1084:88451:11:11:71:539:105:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:that unobserved heterogeneity matters in the sense that estimates of key demand parameters change meaningfully when we omit
25:1085:88574:11:8:72:230:94:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:unobservable heterogeneity from the model.
25:1086:88616:8:15:84:540:86:49:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:20The large difference betweenβandβis in turn driven by the strong relationship between vote shares and the relative circula-
25:1087:88741:15:0:72:167:71:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:tion of Republican papers.
26:1088:88769:0:16:72:540:709:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:the symmetric logit error in the demand system.  The supplemental appendix also presents evidence on the
26:1089:88873:16:15:72:539:693:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:model’s out-of-samplefit to the distribution of subscription prices across market configurations, and to the
26:1090:88981:15:15:72:404:678:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:effect of long-term changes in marginal cost on newspaper market structure.
26:1091:89056:15:16:88:539:663:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:To interpret the magnitude of the parameter estimates and to study the drivers of ideological diversity,
26:1092:89160:16:15:72:540:647:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:table 7 shows the estimated model’s prediction of the level of diversity at baseline and under three counter-
26:1093:89269:15:15:72:540:632:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:factual scenarios.  We measure diversity in three ways:  the number of markets with diverse papers (at least
26:1094:89377:15:16:72:540:617:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:one paper of each type), the share of households in a market with diverse papers, and the share of households
26:1095:89486:16:15:72:242:601:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:reading at least one paper of each type.
26:1096:89526:15:15:88:539:586:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:In ourfirst counterfactual,  we assume that each entering newspaper chooses its affiliation as if it ex-
26:1097:89630:15:16:72:540:571:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:pected to be the only newspaper in the market.  In our second counterfactual, we assume that each newspa-
26:1098:89735:16:15:72:539:555:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:per chooses its affiliation as if its market had equal numbers of Republican and Democratic households.  In
26:1099:89842:15:15:72:539:540:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:our third counterfactual,  we assume that each entering newspaper chooses its affiliation as if there are no
26:1100:89950:15:16:72:540:525:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:idiosyncratic affiliation-specific cost shocksξ. These counterfactuals can be thought of as measuring the im-
26:1101:90059:16:15:71:539:509:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:portance of competition, consumer tastes, and idiosyncratic factors, respectively, in determining equilibrium
26:1102:90168:15:15:71:110:494:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:diversity.
26:1103:90178:15:15:88:539:479:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:Wefind that competition exerts a large effect on diversity: when competitive effects are absent, diversity
26:1104:90284:15:16:71:539:464:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:falls by half.  Eliminating catering to consumer tastes increases diversity by about as much as competition
26:1105:90391:16:32:71:495:448:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:reduces it. Eliminating the role of idiosyncratic factors matters less than eliminating competition.
26:1106:90491:32:22:72:347:416:143:Times:143:Times:C:8.2  Equilibrium and Welfare-Maximizing Outcomes
26:1107:90539:22:15:72:539:394:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:In thefirst column of table 8, we report market structure, prices, and welfare for our baseline model.21As
26:1108:90645:15:16:72:539:379:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:in table 7, each reported value is the average overfive simulations.  We also repeat the baseline diversity
26:1109:90752:16:15:72:415:363:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:statistics from table 7 in thefinal three rows for comparison with what follows.
26:1110:90832:15:15:88:539:348:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:Of  the  951  markets  in  our  baseline  simulation  with  at  least  one  newspaper,  256  have  two  or  more.
26:1111:90945:15:16:72:540:333:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:Thirty-nine percent of households read at least one newspaper.  In multi-paper markets, the average annual
26:1112:91051:16:15:72:540:317:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:subscription price of competitive newspapers is $5.48 (in 1924 dollars), and the average advertising revenue
26:1113:91159:15:15:72:540:302:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:per reader per year is $11.24.  Total surplus is $4.24 per household per year, which breaks down into $3.44
26:1114:91266:15:16:72:412:287:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:of consumer surplus, $0.41 of newspaper profit, and $0.39 of advertiser profit.
26:1115:91345:16:15:88:540:271:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:In thefinal two columns  of table  8,  we compare these  equilibrium  outcomes  to those that  would be
26:1116:91448:15:15:72:540:256:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:chosen by a social planner whose goal is to maximize total surplus. Importantly, we do not assume that the
26:1117:91554:15:16:72:540:241:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:social planner internalizes any political externalities associated with ideological diversity. These simulations
26:1118:91666:16:23:72:540:225:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:therefore allow us to evaluate whether there is any tradeoff between the objectives of maximizing economic
26:1119:91772:23:30:84:468:202:49:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:A:21We define consumer surplus in marketmas total realized utility divided by the marginal utility of money:
26:1120:91878:30:0:71:86:172:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:(15)
26:1121:91882:0:12:281:289:182:49:Times:25:Times:?:Sm
26:1122:91884:12:7:281:290:170:167:g_d0_f8:167:g_d0_f8:C:∑
26:1123:91885:7:0:280:290:163:49:Times:49:g_d0_f1:?:i=1
26:1124:91888:0:25:292:331:172:80:Times:82:g_d0_f8:?:uim(Bi)/α
26:1125:91897:25:11:72:540:147:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:whereBiis the utility-maximizing bundle for householdiandαis the price coefficient in our demand system. We define advertiser
26:1126:92022:11:27:72:423:136:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:surplus in marketmas the total value of advertisements placed less total advertising expenditures:
26:1127:92120:27:0:72:229:109:80:g_d0_f1:49:Times:F:(16)Sm
26:1128:92126:0:16:231:238:125:80:g_d0_f10:80:g_d0_f10:?:-
26:1129:92127:16:0:238:315:109:80:g_d0_f9:80:g_d0_f9:?:(1−q0m)(ah−al)+
26:1130:92142:0:12:318:325:119:49:Times:25:Times:?:Jm
26:1131:92144:12:7:317:326:107:167:g_d0_f8:167:g_d0_f8:C:∑
26:1132:92145:7:0:316:327:100:49:Times:49:g_d0_f1:?:j=1
26:1133:92148:0:0:328:341:109:80:Times:49:Times:?:qjm
26:1134:92151:0:8:342:346:117:80:g_d0_f10:80:g_d0_f10:?:-
26:1135:92152:8:0:346:376:109:80:Times:49:Times:?:al−ajm
26:1136:92158:0:0:376:380:117:80:g_d0_f10:80:g_d0_f10:?:-
26:1137:92159:0:16:381:388:125:80:g_d0_f10:80:g_d0_f10:?:\f
26:1138:92161:16:27:389:391:109:80:g_d0_f5:80:g_d0_f5:?:,
26:1139:92162:27:11:71:540:82:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:whereq0mis the share of households purchasing no newspaper. We define total surplus as the sum of consumer surplus, advertiser
26:1140:92288:11:0:71:182:71:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:surplus, and newspaper profits.
27:1141:92321:0:16:72:335:709:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:welfare and preserving diversity in the marketplace of ideas.
27:1142:92382:16:15:88:539:693:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:The second column of table 8 holds the number of newspapersfixed at baseline values, but allows the
27:1143:92481:15:15:71:540:678:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:social planner  to choose affiliations,  circulation  prices,  and advertising  prices.  Because we estimate that
27:1144:92594:15:16:71:539:663:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:newspapers exercise substantial  market power,  the social planner chooses substantially  lower prices than
27:1145:92701:16:15:71:539:647:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:occur in market equilibrium, with an average price in multi-paper markets of only $0.04, leading the share
27:1146:92807:15:15:71:539:632:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:of households reading newspapers to increase by about half.  As in Steiner (1952), the social planner also
27:1147:92913:15:16:71:539:617:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:chooses more ideological diversity than occurs in market equilibrium:  the number of markets with diverse
27:1148:93018:16:15:71:539:601:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:papers increases from 143 to 175.  This occurs because newspapers  do not capture the full surplus from
27:1149:93121:15:15:71:539:586:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:greater diversity.  We show in the supplemental appendix that this distortion is most important in markets
27:1150:93227:15:16:71:539:571:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:in which consumers’ affiliations are about evenly split.  The combined effect of the reduction in prices and
27:1151:93335:16:15:71:539:555:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:the increase in the diversity of newspapers is to increase the share of households reading diverse papers by
27:1152:93443:15:15:71:143:540:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:a factor of three.
27:1153:93461:15:15:88:539:525:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:The third column of table 8 allows the social planner to control newspapers’ entry decisions as well as
27:1154:93564:15:16:71:539:510:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:post-entry outcomes. The results show that in market equilibrium the number of newspapers falls well short
27:1155:93670:16:15:71:539:494:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:of the social optimum. The social planner increases the number of markets with at least one paper from 951
27:1156:93776:15:15:71:539:479:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:to 1910 and the number of markets with multiple papers from 256 to 1845. Increased entry further increases
27:1157:93882:15:16:71:539:464:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:diversity:  the number of households  in markets with diverse papers rises to 84 percent,  and one-third of
27:1158:93989:16:15:71:285:448:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:households read diverse papers on any given day.
27:1159:94037:15:15:88:539:433:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:The source of insufficient  entry here is the distortion  formalized  by Spence (1975):  in markets with
27:1160:94141:15:16:71:540:418:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f1:B:fixed costs, entrants do not internalize the effect of entry on the surplus of inframarginal consumers.22The
27:1161:94249:16:15:72:540:402:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:result  is not mechanical.   In the standard  symmetric logit model,  which our model nests as a limit case,
27:1162:94357:15:15:72:539:387:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:the number offirms in the free entry equilibrium can be greater or fewer than thefirst-best (Anderson et
27:1163:94461:15:16:72:540:372:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:al.  1992).  Insufficient  entry arises at the estimated  parameters because  consumers capture a large share
27:1164:94570:16:15:72:539:356:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:of surplus and because the significant (and empirically realistic) amount of multiple readership means the
27:1165:94676:15:15:72:540:341:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:business-stealing externality highlighted in Mankiw and Whinson (1986) is relatively small. This contrasts
27:1166:94782:15:16:72:540:326:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:with the results of Berry and Waldfogel (1999) for radio, where the estimated business-stealing externality
27:1167:94889:16:15:72:373:310:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:is large, and equilibrium entry is consequently found to be excessive.
27:1168:94959:15:15:88:539:295:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:Because the use of a symmetric logit error is known to exaggerate the benefits from additional variety, in
27:1169:95065:15:15:72:540:280:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:the supplemental appendix we show that the gains from moving to the social optimum are large even when
27:1170:95167:15:16:72:353:265:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:we severely cap the number of potential entrants to each market.
27:1171:95231:16:15:88:540:249:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:The results in table 8 show that there is no conflict between the goal of maximizing economic welfare
27:1172:95332:15:15:72:540:234:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:and the goal of maintaining diversity in the marketplace of ideas.  Policies that increase entry,  as well as
27:1173:95441:15:16:72:540:219:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:policies which promote diversity conditional on entry, would likely increase economic welfare even if the
27:1174:95546:16:31:72:268:203:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:political externalities to diversity were small.
27:1175:95594:31:23:71:197:172:143:Times:143:Times:C:8.3  Policy Experiments
27:1176:95617:23:15:71:539:149:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:Table 9 presents a series of policy experiments based on the model. Thefirst column shows baseline results
27:1177:95723:15:48:71:539:134:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:for reference.  The second through fourth columns relax competition policy by allowing newspapers to col-
27:1178:95828:48:15:84:539:86:49:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:22For early discussions of the tendency toward inefficient entry in concentrated markets see Hotelling (1938) and the work of
27:1179:95953:15:0:71:317:71:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:Jules Dupuit as summarized in Ekelund and Hebert (1999, 159-191).
28:1180:96020:0:16:72:539:709:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:lude on prices only, advertising only, and both prices and advertising.23In all of these columns newspapers
28:1181:96127:16:15:72:539:693:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:continue to make non-cooperative entry and affiliation decisions.  Arrangements in which newspapers col-
28:1182:96231:15:15:72:540:678:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:lude on prices and advertising but remain editorially separate are called “joint operating agreements” and
28:1183:96337:15:16:72:399:663:119:g_d0_f1:64:g_d0_f1:B:have existed in the United States since 1933 (Busterna and Picard, 1993).24
28:1184:96412:16:15:88:539:647:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:Allowing price collusion  reduces economic welfare and has little effect  on diversity.   Average prices
28:1185:96516:15:15:71:539:632:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:in multi-paper  markets rise significantly,  from $5.48 to $7.53.   Advertising  revenue  per reader  increases
28:1186:96627:15:16:71:539:617:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:slightly,  as a consequence  of less overlap in newspaper  readership.   The number of markets with two or
28:1187:96733:16:15:71:539:601:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:more newspapers rises modestly from 256 to 290. Most of the gain to newspapers is offset by this increase
28:1188:96838:15:15:71:539:586:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:in competitiveness, so total newspaper profit increases only slightly, while consumer surplus and advertiser
28:1189:96946:15:16:71:539:571:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:profit both fall.  Additional entry also offsets the reduced incentive to differentiate due to softer price com-
28:1190:97058:16:15:71:539:555:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:petition, and so effects on diversity are modest:  the share of households with access to diverse papers rises
28:1191:97168:15:15:71:258:540:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:slightly, while the share reading them falls.
28:1192:97213:15:15:88:539:525:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:Advertising collusion, on the other hand, causes large increases in both economic welfare and diversity.
28:1193:97317:15:16:71:539:510:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:Because our baseline estimates imply significant competition in the advertising market (al<ah), advertising
28:1194:97424:16:15:72:539:494:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:collusion increases advertising revenue per reader from $11.24 to $12.14.  The increase in advertising rev-
28:1195:97531:15:15:72:540:479:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:enue leads newspapers to reduce circulation prices to consumers, consistent with the well-known “seesaw
28:1196:97634:15:16:72:540:464:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:principle” in two-sided markets (Rochet and Tirole 2006; Dewenter et al.  2011).  Entry increases dramati-
28:1197:97740:16:15:72:540:448:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:cally, with the number of markets with multiple papers going from 256 to 400. These factors together cause
28:1198:97846:15:15:72:539:433:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:consumer  surplus to increase  significantly,  and total  surplus  increases  increases  from $4.24 to $4.90 per
28:1199:97958:15:16:72:540:418:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:household per year.  The large increase in entry more than offsets the reduced incentive to differentiate due
28:1200:98067:16:15:72:454:402:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:to reduced advertising competition, and so diversity rises by about half on all measures.
28:1201:98156:15:15:88:539:387:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:Joint operating agreements combine the effects of price and advertising collusion.  The effects of ad-
28:1202:98258:15:16:72:540:372:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:vertising collusion dominate the effect on diversity, which remains positive on all measures.  The two types
28:1203:98366:16:15:72:540:356:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:of collusion essentially cancel in terms of welfare impact, with a small net gain in total surplus relative to
28:1204:98476:15:15:72:110:341:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:baseline.
28:1205:98485:15:16:88:539:326:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:An important take-away from these results is that the two-sided nature of media markets substantially
28:1206:98586:16:15:72:540:310:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:changes the evaluation of policy instruments. Price and advertising collusion are frequently treated as sym-
28:1207:98694:15:26:72:540:295:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:metric in the policy debate,25while in fact the two are very different.  Joint setting of prices amounts to a
28:1208:98803:26:30:84:422:269:49:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:A:23We define a collusive price of newspaperjas thejthelement of a price vectorp∗that solves
28:1209:98893:30:7:72:261:239:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:(17)p∗∈arg max
28:1210:98907:7:0:245:249:232:49:Times:49:Times:?:p
28:1211:98908:0:11:264:271:249:49:Times:25:Times:?:Jm
28:1212:98910:11:8:263:272:238:167:g_d0_f8:167:g_d0_f8:C:∑

28:1215:98915:8:0:279:354:239:80:Times:80:Times:?:pj+ajm(p,τ)−MC
28:1216:98929:0:8:354:358:247:80:g_d0_f10:80:g_d0_f10:?:-

28:1219:99009:27:0:72:255:186:80:g_d0_f1:49:Times:F:(18)ajm=ah
28:1220:99019:0:9:257:264:201:80:g_d0_f10:80:g_d0_f10:?:-
28:1221:99020:9:14:268:295:192:80:g_d0_f1:49:Times:?:1−q0m
28:1222:99025:14:0:265:297:178:80:g_d0_f8:49:Times:?:∑Jmk=1qkm
28:1223:99034:0:15:299:306:201:80:g_d0_f10:80:g_d0_f10:?:\f
28:1224:99036:15:0:308:322:186:80:g_d0_f9:49:Times:?:+al
28:1225:99039:0:15:324:331:201:80:g_d0_f10:80:g_d0_f10:?:-
28:1226:99040:15:8:331:376:186:80:g_d0_f1:49:Times:?:1−1−q0m
28:1227:99047:8:0:346:379:178:80:g_d0_f8:49:Times:?:∑Jmk=1qkm
28:1228:99056:0:41:381:388:201:80:g_d0_f10:80:g_d0_f10:?:\f
28:1229:99058:41:8:72:298:160:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:whereq0mis the share of households purchasing no newspaper.
28:1230:99117:8:14:84:539:152:49:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:24We assume that papers in joint operating agreements keep all of their own subscription revenue and that they share advertising
28:1231:99245:14:11:71:539:138:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:revenue in proportion to their circulations. These assumptions are a reasonable match to the revenue-sharing arrangements of joint
28:1232:99375:11:11:71:539:127:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:operating agreements  authorized under the Newspaper  Preservation Act of 1970 (Busterna and Picard,  1993).  In some cases a
28:1233:99500:11:11:72:540:116:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:newspaper’s share of revenue is a “sliding” function of the newspaper’s contribution to revenue or to total advertising sales.  In
28:1234:99630:11:11:72:540:105:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:other cases, the revenue sharing rule isfixed in advance, but in such cases is usually related to the initial capital investment of the
28:1235:99765:11:11:72:540:94:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:newspapers, and hence to theirfinancial health at the time of the agreement.  In both types of arrangements, a newspaper with a
28:1236:99892:11:8:72:402:83:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:greater circulation will generally be entitled to a greater share of the joint venture’s revenue.
28:1237:99989:8:0:84:539:75:49:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:25See, e.g., the discussion of the debate surrounding the Newspaper Preservation Act of 1970 in Oppenheim and Shields (1981,
29:1238:100115:0:16:72:540:709:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:tax on marginal readership and only a modest spur to entry, while joint setting of advertising rates amounts
29:1239:100223:16:15:72:540:693:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:to a subsidy to marginal readership and a massive spur to entry.  In a world where entry,  readership,  and
29:1240:100330:15:15:72:540:678:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:diversity are all inefficiently low, permitting advertising collusion may be a surprisingly attractive policy to
29:1241:100442:15:16:72:381:663:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:a regulator concerned with both economic welfare and diversity per se.
29:1242:100512:16:15:88:539:647:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:In thefifth column of table 9, we evaluate the effect of relaxing ownership regulation by assuming that all
29:1243:100619:15:15:71:539:632:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:newspapers in a market are jointly owned. Federal oversight of broadcast media ownership began in the US
29:1244:100723:15:16:71:539:617:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:with the Communications Act of 1934 (Candeub 2007) and continues today.26We model joint ownership
29:1245:100820:16:15:71:539:601:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:by assuming that entering newspapers set collusive circulation and advertising prices as in joint operating
29:1246:100927:15:15:71:539:586:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:agreements, that the number of entering newspapers is chosen to maximize total expected newspaper profits,
29:1247:101033:15:16:71:539:571:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:and that newspapers choose affiliations to maximize total newspaper profits subject to a common affiliation-
29:1248:101141:16:15:71:174:555:119:g_d0_f1:64:g_d0_f1:B:specific cost shockξ.27
29:1249:101164:15:15:88:539:540:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:Joint ownership significantly reduces welfare, diversity, and the number of newspapers. Circulation and
29:1250:101267:15:16:71:539:525:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:advertising prices both rise, and newspaper readership falls.  Most of the drop in diversity is a consequence
29:1251:101376:16:15:71:539:509:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:of reduced entry; the share of multi-paper markets with diverse papers remains roughly stable. This reflects
29:1252:101484:15:15:71:539:494:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:two offsetting effects on differentiation.  On the one hand, allowing newspapers to internalize the effect of
29:1253:101593:15:15:71:539:479:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:their affiliation choices on their competitors significantly increases the incentive to differentiate (Sweeting
29:1254:101704:15:16:72:540:464:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:2010).  On the other hand, the fact that we assume jointly owned newspapers share a common cost shock
29:1255:101805:16:15:72:540:448:121:g_d0_f8:119:g_d0_f1:B:ξsignificantly increases the within-market correlation of affiliation choices, providing a strong force in the
29:1256:101915:15:15:72:138:433:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:other direction.
29:1257:101931:15:16:88:539:418:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:In the sixth andfinal column of table 9 we evaluate a marginal cost subsidy to newspapers. In the 1920s,
29:1258:102035:16:15:72:540:402:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:postal subsidies offset a meaningful fraction of newspaper delivery costs for many newspapers (Kielbowicz
29:1259:102140:15:15:72:539:387:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:1994).  We allow the government to transferKdollars per newspaper sold to the newspaper’s owner, at a
29:1260:102241:15:16:71:539:372:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:cost of(1+λ)per dollar transferred.  We set the marginal costλof public funds to 0.3 (Einav et al.  2010;
29:1261:102346:16:15:72:383:356:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:Poterba 1996). We compute the level ofKthat maximizes total surplus.
29:1262:102414:15:15:88:539:341:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:The surplus-maximizing marginal cost subsidy amounts to an average payment of $4.00 per copy per
29:1263:102510:15:16:72:540:326:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:year, equivalent to a 49 percent reduction in marginal cost. For comparison, the US postal subsidy amounted
29:1264:102617:16:15:72:539:310:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:to a roughly 12 percent reduction in marginal cost.28Of all the policies we consider, this one is the most
29:1265:102723:15:15:72:540:295:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:effective in increasing economic welfare and diversity, both because it promotes entry in markets that previ-
29:1266:102832:15:38:72:480:280:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:ously had no papers, and because it increases readership conditional on the number of papers.
29:1267:102925:38:26:72:167:242:206:Times:206:Times:C:9  Conclusions
29:1268:102939:26:16:72:539:216:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:We estimate a model of newspapers’  entry and choice of political affiliation that matches key facts from
29:1269:103044:16:29:72:540:200:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:novel data on US daily newspapers in 1924.  We use the model to evaluate the economic determinants of
29:1270:103145:29:8:72:107:171:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:187-189).
29:1271:103154:8:14:84:539:163:49:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:26For example, in the US today, the FCC limits ownership of a daily newspaper and a TV or radio station in the same local
29:1272:103275:14:11:71:539:149:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:market, as well as ownership of multiple radio or television stations in the same market.  Direct regulation of newpaper ownership
29:1273:103405:11:11:71:540:138:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:is less common, though it does exist. In France, for example, no newspaper acquisition will be approved if the combined entity will
29:1274:103536:11:8:71:303:127:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:have a circulation share greater than 30 percent (McEwen 2007).
29:1275:103599:8:14:84:539:119:49:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:27That is, we assume thatξjm-τjm-=ξj-m-τj-m-∀j,j-s.t.τjm=τj-m. We continue to assume that the draw onξis not known
29:1276:103713:14:8:72:515:105:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:at the entry stage, and compute the expected valuesV(J)by numerically integrating over theξvia Monte Carlo simulation.
29:1277:103831:8:15:84:539:97:49:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:28In 1924, the post office’s cost of publication delivery exceeded its revenue by a factor of more than three (Kielbowicz 1994).
29:1278:103959:15:11:72:540:82:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:We estimate that postage accounted for 6 percent of variable costs, so the implicit subsidy was approximately 12 percent of variable
29:1279:104091:11:0:71:91:71:80:g_d0_f1:80:g_d0_f1:F:costs.
30:1280:104099:0:16:72:540:709:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:ideological diversity and to evaluate several important policies.  Wefind that competitive incentives are a
30:1281:104206:16:15:72:539:693:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:crucial driver of ideological  diversity.  We show that there is no conflict between the goal of maximizing
30:1282:104313:15:15:72:539:678:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:economic welfare and the goal of preserving ideological diversity. Wefind that accounting for the two-sided
30:1283:104420:15:16:72:540:663:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:nature of the market is critical for evaluating competition policies, in that permitting advertising collusion
30:1284:104530:16:15:72:540:647:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:increases  both  welfare  and  diversity,  whereas  permitting  price  collusion  reduces  welfare  and  has  mixed
30:1285:104645:15:15:72:540:632:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:effects on diversity.  We evaluate other prominent media policies such as ownership regulation and explicit
30:1286:104752:15:38:72:114:617:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:subsidies.

42:1693:131285:26:16:72:540:644:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:In appendix tables 1 and 2, we show how our key results vary with alternative specifications of the model.
42:1694:131391:16:15:72:539:628:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:Appendix table 1 reports, for each specification and counterfactual, the share of households reading at least
42:1695:131500:15:15:72:539:613:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:one paper of each affiliation, averaged overfive simulations.  Appendix table 2 reports, for each specifica-
42:1696:131608:15:16:72:539:598:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:tion and counterfactual, the total surplus per household, averaged overfive simulations.  Each table hasfive
42:1697:131716:16:15:72:539:582:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:columns. Thefirst column reports results for the baseline model. The second column reports results assum-
42:1698:131821:15:15:72:540:567:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:ing that the social planner chooses all entry and post-entry decisions as in thefinal column of table 8.  The
42:1699:131930:15:16:72:540:552:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:third and fourth columns report results with joint operating agreements and joint ownership, respectively, as
42:1700:132039:16:15:72:540:536:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:in the fourth andfifth columns of table 9.  Thefifth column reports results assuming the optimal marginal
42:1701:132144:15:15:72:488:521:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:cost subsidy is in place, at the value computed for the case shown in thefinal column of table 9.
42:1702:132241:15:15:93:539:506:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:Thefirst row of the table repeats the results from our main specification for reference.  In parenthe-
42:1703:132343:15:16:72:540:491:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:ses, we show standard errors for each counterfactual,  computed as the standard deviation across 5 sets of
42:1704:132449:16:15:72:540:475:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:parameters, each drawn from the asymptotic (joint) distribution of the demand and supply parameters.  In
42:1705:132553:15:15:72:540:460:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:brackets, we show the simulation error for each counterfactual, computed as the standard deviation across 5
42:1706:132660:15:0:72:324:445:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:simulation draws from the baseline parameters divided by

42:1709:132719:16:15:93:539:429:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:A:The second through ninth rows explore changes to the moments we use to calibrate parameters in the
42:1710:132817:15:15:72:540:414:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:model.  In each case we change a single moment (increasing or decreasing by 25 percent), re-estimate the
42:1711:132921:15:16:72:539:399:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:model, and recompute counterfactuals.  The second and third specifications show results for the marginal
42:1712:133025:16:15:72:539:383:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:cost. The fourth andfifth specifications show results for monopoly advertising revenue per reader. The sixth
42:1713:133133:15:15:72:539:368:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:and  seventh  specifications  show  results  for  the  average  overlap  in  readership  among  different-affiliation
42:1714:133250:15:16:72:540:353:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:newspapers.   These changes leave our key qualitative  conclusions  unchanged.   Not surprisingly,  as these
42:1715:133358:16:15:72:539:337:119:g_d0_f1:119:g_d0_f1:B:parameters directly affect the economic efficiency of newspaper readership, changing them has some quan-
